    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";

          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer4753513341778143803">And Quiet Flows the Don or Quietly Flows the Don (Тихий Дон, lit. "The Quiet Don") is 4-volume epic novel by Russian writer Mikhail Aleksandrovich Sholokhov. The 1st three volumes were written from 1925 to '32 & published in the Soviet magazine October in 1928–32. The 4th volume was finished in 1940. The English translation of the 1st three volumes appeared under this titl</span>
  <span id="freeText4753513341778143803" style="display:none">And Quiet Flows the Don or Quietly Flows the Don (Тихий Дон, lit. "The Quiet Don") is 4-volume epic novel by Russian writer Mikhail Aleksandrovich Sholokhov. The 1st three volumes were written from 1925 to '32 &amp; published in the Soviet magazine October in 1928–32. The 4th volume was finished in 1940. The English translation of the 1st three volumes appeared under this title in 1934. The novel is considered one of the most significant works of Russian literature in the 20th century. It depicts the lives &amp; struggles of Don Cossacks during WWI, the Russian Revolution &amp; Russian Civil War. In 1965, Sholokhov was awarded the Nobel Prize for Literature for this novel. The authorship of the novel is contested by some literary critics &amp; historians, who believe it wasn't entirely written by Sholokhov.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4753513341778143803" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/802444289" />
    <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani"><img alt="Ahmad Sharabiani" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1194930432p2/614778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/802444289?book_show_action=true">Dec 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ahmad Sharabiani" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani">Ahmad Sharabiani</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/7686955">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer802444289" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3531188402841250061">Tiknii Don = Quiet Flows the Don: a novel in Four books, Mikhail Sholokhov<br/>And Quiet Flows the Don or Quietly Flows the Don (Тихий Дон, literally "Quiet Don") is an epic novel in four volumes by Russian writer Mikhail Aleksandrovich Sholokhov. The first three volumes were written from 1925 to 1932 and published in the Soviet magazine Oktyabr in 1928–1932, and the fourth volume was finished in 1940. The English translation of the first three volumes appeared under this title in 1934.<br/>عنوانها: دن آر</span>
  <span id="freeText3531188402841250061" style="display:none">Tiknii Don = Quiet Flows the Don: a novel in Four books, Mikhail Sholokhov<br />And Quiet Flows the Don or Quietly Flows the Don (Тихий Дон, literally "Quiet Don") is an epic novel in four volumes by Russian writer Mikhail Aleksandrovich Sholokhov. The first three volumes were written from 1925 to 1932 and published in the Soviet magazine Oktyabr in 1928–1932, and the fourth volume was finished in 1940. The English translation of the first three volumes appeared under this title in 1934.<br />عنوانها: دن آرام - چهار جلدی؛ دن در دریا آرام میگیرد؛ دن آرام - چهار جلد در دو مجلد؛ نویسنده: میخائیل شولوخوف؛ تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز هشتم ماه مارس سال 1977 میلادی<br />عنوان: دن آرام - چهار جلدی؛ نویسنده: میخائیل شولوخوف؛ مترجم: م.ا. به آذین (محمود اعتمادزاده)؛ تهران، نیل، 1344؛ در چهار جلد؛ چاپ دوم 1356؛ چاپ دیگر: 1363؛ چاپ دیگر: 1381 در چهار جلد و 1760 ص؛ شابک دوره: 9647294549؛ چاپ دیگر: تهران، فردوس؛ 1388؛<br /> در چهار جلد؛ شابک: 9789643204099؛ موضوع: داستان جنگهای داخلی روسیه و انقلاب در روسیه، قرن 20 م<br />عنوان: دن آرام - چهار جلد در دو مجلد؛ نویسنده: میخائیل شولوخوف؛ مترجم: احمد شاملو؛ تهران، مازیار، 1382؛ شابک: 9789645676276؛<br />عنوان: دن آرام - چهار جلد در دو مجلد؛ نویسنده: میخائیل شولوخوف؛ مترجم: منوچهر بیگدلی خمسه؛ تهران، گلشائی؛ 1368؛ در چهار جلد؛<br />عنوان: دن در دریا آرام میگیرد؛ مترجم: اسماعیل قهرمانی پور؛ تهران، سمیر، 1391؛ در 1800 ص؛ شابک: 9789642201655؛<br />چکیده: خانواده ی ملخوف، اولاد «پروکوفی ملخوف (مه له خوف)» هستند؛ که از جنگ‌های عثمانی، زنی ترک تبار بنام «مورا» به خانه آورده بود. گریگوری دومین فرزند، و دومین پسر، و آخرین پسر خانواده‌ است. او دلباخته ی زن همسایه، به نام: آکسیانا می‌شود. آکسیانا، که از شوهری تندخو و بدرفتار خود «استپان آستاخوف»، بیزار است، متقابلاً به گریگوری دل می‌بازد، و با او رابطه برقرار می‌کند. وقتی پدر گریگوری، به رابطه ی نامشروع این دو پی می‌برد، به اجبار گریگوری را، به ازدواج با دختری پاکدامن، به نام: «ناتالیا»، وامی‌دارد؛ ولی گریگوری دل در گرو عشق آکسیانا دارد، و با زن قانونی خود، به سردی برخورد می‌کند. آنگاه وقت آن می‌رسد، که گریگوری کار در مزرعه را رها کند، و به خدمت نیروهای نظامی روسیه، اعزام شود. او می‌رود، و خیلی زود، جنگ جهانگیر نخست آغاز می‌شود. او در جنگ به مناسبت شجاعتش، صلیب سن ژرژ می‌گیرد، و به درجه ی استواری، و سپس به درجه ی افسری می‌رسد. با پیروزی اولیه نیروهای شورشی و ارتش سفید، ژنرال‌های سفید، گریگوری را به مناسبت سواد کمش از فرماندهی لشکر (ژنرالی - بدون درجه)، به ستوانی (درجه‌ ای که پیشتر داشت) تنزل می‌دهند، و گریگوری می‌پذیرد. اما چندی بعد به صف بلشویک‌ها می‌پیوندد، و در نبردی با آنها، همقطار می‌گردد. انقلاب بلشویک فرا می‌رسد، و سربازان در دو دسته ی سفیدها و سرخها، با هم مبارزه می‌کنند و به کشتن یکدیگر مبادرت می‌ورزند. حوادث، موافق نیروهای شورشی، و ارتش سفید نیست. آنها در برابر کمونیست‌ها شکست می‌خورند، و عقب می‌نشینند، و فرماندهانشان می‌گریزند. گریگوری وقتی می‌بیند سفیدها و سرخها چگونه اسیران یکدیگر را که هموطن هم هستند می‌کشند، دلزده و دلخسته عزم خانه و کاشانه می‌کند. گریگوری به خانه می‌آید، در حالیکه پدرش، مادرش، برادرش، زن برادرش، پدرزنش، و همسر قانونی اش را، در طول داستان از دست داده‌ است. او امیدوار است که باز سر ِ زمین برود، و زندگی آرامی را به دور از جنگ و ویرانی، و آدمکشی آغاز کند. اما غافل از اینکه هم‌ولایتی، و دوست سابقش: «میخائیل کاشه ووی»، که از ابتدا به سرخها پیوسته، و هوادار متعصب و سرسخت کمونیست‌ها شده بود، و خواهر او را نیز به زنی گرفته، و در منزل آنها ساکن شده بود - قصد دارد او را به این خاطر که ضدانقلابی است دستگیر و از بین ببرد. گریگوری می‌گریزد و چند ماه با دسته‌ ای یاغی همگام می‌شود. اما در نهایت برمیگردد، و دلداده ی خود، آکسیانا را برمی‌دارد، و رو به سوی مقصدی (زندگی آرام) می‌رود. آکسیانا در این راه، توسط نگهبانان سرخ کشته می‌شود، و گریگوری او را دفن می‌کند، و از آنجا می‌رود. گریگوری که همه چیزش را باخته‌ است، به خانه برمیگردد، و درمی‌یابد که دخترش نیز از بیماری جان سپرده‌ است. گریگوری پس از آن، به آبادی مزرعه ی خویش می‌پردازد، و زمان می‌گذرد، و پسرش میشاتکا ازدواج می‌کند، و برخلاف پدر، خانواده‌ ای سرشار از عشق و شادی تشکیل می‌دهد. زمان می‌گذرد ولی دن همچنان آرام به مسیر خود به سوی دریا ادامه می‌دهد. ...؛ نخست برگردان «به آذین» را خواندم، سپس برگردانی از روسی به انگلیسی که نام مترجمش یادم نمانده است، سپس برگردان احمد شاملو را. هر سه برایم گوارا بودند. ا. شربیانی</span>
  <a data-text-id="3531188402841250061" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_143384263" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/143384263" />
    <a title="El" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83144-el"><img alt="El" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1479156984p2/83144.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/143384263?book_show_action=true">Jan 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="El" class="user" itemprop="url" name="El" href="/user/show/83144-el">El</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780786703609" href="/book/show/1076385.Quiet_Flows_the_Don">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=20th-centurylit-early">20th-centurylit-early</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=russia">russia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=big-effing-books">big-effing-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer143384263" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12348919456312320691">I initially thought reading this would take longer than a month, but somehow I managed to rock its Russian face. It's no secret that these Russian tomes can often be dense, filled with hundreds of characters (with a bunch of different names) doing things in highly detailed settings, where things like a blade of grass or a pebble in a river somehow manage to be a character themselves for fifty pages or so. Russian novels are dark and cold, just like the land from whence they came. Many of the aut</span>
  <span id="freeText12348919456312320691" style="display:none">I initially thought reading this would take longer than a month, but somehow I managed to rock its Russian face. It's no secret that these Russian tomes can often be dense, filled with hundreds of characters (with a bunch of different names) doing things in highly detailed settings, where things like a blade of grass or a pebble in a river somehow manage to be a character themselves for fifty pages or so. Russian novels are dark and cold, just like the land from whence they came. Many of the authors had beards, even the women authors! (Or so I like to imagine.)<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380344628i/699513.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>(Sholokhov, sadly, did not have a beard. At one point he had a mustache, but that hardly counts.)<br><br><i>Quiet Flows the Don</i> is no exception in the land of Dark and Cold Russian Novels. This book has girth, baby. The Don of the title refers to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Don_River_%28Russia%29" rel="nofollow noopener">Don River</a> and it most certainly is a character in the book. It has attitude, and apparently emotions. (Okay, so in reality? The attitude and emotions of the human characters are reflected in the motion of the river. It's all very literary, yo. Symbolic and shit.)<br><br>The story starts in the early 20th century, before WWI and all, and it revolves around a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cossack" rel="nofollow noopener">Cossack</a> family. Did I mention there were a lot of characters? There were. According to the List of Characters at the beginning of the book (to which I referred regularly) there are 32 main characters, 53 Historical Persons including some actual familiar names like Stalin, Lenin, and Trotsky. I suck at math, but I do know that the grand total then comes out to 85 characters. (And maybe more that the List of Characters didn't think necessary to mention?)<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380344628i/699514._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>(That's a Cossack. Burly, right?)<br><br>So this Sholokhov guy. He was a fanboy of Tolstoy, which I think is pretty evident in <i>Quiet Flows the Don</i>. I would say Sholokhov wrote this book with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/656.War_and_Peace" title="War and Peace by Leo Tolstoy" rel="nofollow noopener">War and Peace</a> in mind. Like <i>War and Peace</i>, <i>Quiet Flows the Don</i> is about war - though instead of focusing on the French invasion of Russia as in the Tolstoy, Soholokhov's story revolved around the start of WWI and Russian Civil War. There are periods of "peace" in both books as well, moments of downtime in which we see a flash of home life - the women, the children, the farms - though the politics never seems to leave these pages. This is a politically charged novel, and Sholokhov's personal politics are on every single page. (Yes, that <i>can</i> get tedious, thank you for asking.)<br><br>But I'm happy I read this. As far as the Russkies go, Sholokhov is one I haven't heard that much about. Apparently he and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/10420.Aleksandr_Solzhenitsyn" title="Aleksandr Solzhenitsyn" rel="nofollow noopener">Aleksandr Solzhenitsyn</a> had a few issues which I think is interesting. I mean, Solzhenitsyn looked like he could kick a few asses. (Or at least smother people to death in his <a href="http://cache1.asset-cache.net/xc/50419960.jpg?v=1&amp;c=IWSAsset&amp;k=2&amp;d=E41C9FE5C4AA0A149994BA46E381C7D82AD25A20B8E3B59C2B8B04A3F2271072B01E70F2B3269972" rel="nofollow noopener">beard</a>.) I can't say that Sholokhov was as good as his forefathers like Tolstoy, or even some who came slightly later, like Solzhenitsyn. But a fine contribution to Russian literature, and a solid good read nonetheless.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12348919456312320691" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/544877497?book_show_action=true">Sep 21, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=russia">russia</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=humor">humor</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=ww1">ww1</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=hf">hf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=2019-read">2019-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer544877497" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7158463740268144018">In 1965 <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/44202.Mikhail_Sholokhov' title='Mikhail Sholokhov' rel='nofollow noopener'>Mikhail Sholokhov</a> was awarded the Nobel Prize for Literature for <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/78024.And_Quiet_Flows_the_Don' title='And Quiet Flows the Don by Mikhail Sholokhov' rel='nofollow noopener'>And Quiet Flows the Don</a>. It is a lengthy novel about the Cossack people covering a time period starting from the end of the Russo-Turkish War of 1877-1878 through the First World War, the Russian Revolution and the Russian Civil War. <br/><br/>The English language Audible version of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/78024.And_Quiet_Flows_the_Don' title='And Quiet Flows the Don by Mikhail Sholokhov' rel='nofollow noopener'>And Quiet Flows the Don</a> is 13 hours long, <u>un</u>abridged, read by Stefan Rudnicki but is only the <u>first</u> of the book's four volumes. The following three volum</span>
  <span id="freeText7158463740268144018" style="display:none">In 1965 <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/44202.Mikhail_Sholokhov" title="Mikhail Sholokhov" rel="nofollow noopener">Mikhail Sholokhov</a> was awarded the Nobel Prize for Literature for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/78024.And_Quiet_Flows_the_Don" title="And Quiet Flows the Don by Mikhail Sholokhov" rel="nofollow noopener">And Quiet Flows the Don</a>. It is a lengthy novel about the Cossack people covering a time period starting from the end of the Russo-Turkish War of 1877-1878 through the First World War, the Russian Revolution and the Russian Civil War. <br /><br />The English language Audible version of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/78024.And_Quiet_Flows_the_Don" title="And Quiet Flows the Don by Mikhail Sholokhov" rel="nofollow noopener">And Quiet Flows the Don</a> is 13 hours long, <u>un</u>abridged, read by Stefan Rudnicki but is only the <u>first</u> of the book's four volumes. The following three volumes, here given in order, are entitled <u>The Don Flows Down to the Sea</u>, <u>Virgin Soil Upturned</u> and <u>Harvest on the Don</u>. The first volume, which is as far as I have come and review here, ends during the First World War.<br /><br />Three things stand out and have made the novel special for me. <br /><br />I like the author’s prose style, a lot. I will provide one quote:<br /><br />“And over the village slipped the days, passing into the nights; the weeks flowed by, the months crept on, the wind howled, and, glassified with an autumnal, translucent, greenish-azure, the Don flowed tranquilly down to the sea.”<br /><br /><br />I like the words the author chooses. The language is not florid, it is simple and strong. It encompasses an undercurrent of feeling. The author speaks of the land, of the river, the wind, and the Cossack people. He pays attention to the basics—how the dirt sucks up the dew, the howling of the wind, the colors, the majesty and the elemental beauty of the steppes and the river. <br /><br />Just as he magnificently draws the landscape, he captures the essence of the Cossack people--their passion, their hot-blooded nature and turbulent spirit. Their sense of being one of a group. I have read of the Cossacks in many books, but know I know them. This is the second reason I so very much like this book. <br /><br />My appreciation of the prose must in part be due to the book’s translator, Stephen Garry. <br /><br />Thirdly, how the author constructs the novel is well done. Being essentially about a group of people, rather than particular individuals, he ties the many to a few central characters, with the Melekhov family of Tatarsk at the center. Tatarsk is a village of the Don River valley. The family consists of the father, mother, two sons and a daughter, who is the youngest. The elder son is married. The younger son, Grigori Melekhov*, is the primary protagonist of the story. He loves the wife of the son in a neighboring family. This is a scandal in the village. It is not to be accepted. He is alternately guffawed and scorned and then pushed into marriage. His wife does love him, but he doesn’t love her. One cannot negate that which one feels. Above all else, the Cossacks are an emotional people. <br /><br />I will give only example of how the many, many characters and diverse stories connect back to Grigore. He is off at war, the First World War, and he is in the cavalry. Horses are galloping, sabers and lances fly. We are introduced to a new character, a young, newly enlisted Cossack recruit, a student of mathematics. He is writing a diary. He is in love….he is disappointed in love and then he dies. His notebook comes into the hands of Grigori. By throwing in this new character, readers are given another figure, another Cossack, another soldier of the war. With this little story, humor and sadness come together. It had me first smiling and then sighing. The multitude of individuals tied to the few central characters allows readers to grasp the Cossacks as a group, as a people, as a whole, made up of different personality types, people of different ages and experiences. The author does this very well; he knows what to put in and what to leave out. <br /><br />Stefan Rudnicki narrates the audiobook. I like very much how he reads it, so four stars for the narration. He speaks clearly and with strength. He keeps a good pace. His pronunciation of Russian names allows one to easily distinguished one from the other. <br /><br />I hope the remaining three volumes will soon be available at Audible, and I hope they too will be read by Stefan Rudnicki.<br /><br /><br />* Grigori Melekhov is said to be based on two real people, two anti-Bolshevik leaders of the Upper Don.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7158463740268144018" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_653408122" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/653408122" />
    <a title="Bill Purkayastha" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21147300-bill-purkayastha"><img alt="Bill Purkayastha" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1370765830p2/21147300.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/653408122?book_show_action=true">Jun 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bill Purkayastha" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bill Purkayastha" href="/user/show/21147300-bill-purkayastha">Bill Purkayastha</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer653408122" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5721314962282062980">How does one go about writing a review of one’s favourite book of all? One runs the risk of making one of two errors: one might either ignore flaws, and praise too much, to the point of gushing; or, one might try and be too critical in an attempt to be even-handed, and thus sell the work short.<br/><br/>Mikhail Sholokhov’s Quiet Flows the Don (that’s the complete and unabridged English edition of Tikhii Don, “The Quiet Don” in the original Russian) is not an easy book to read. It’s not something for those</span>
  <span id="freeText5721314962282062980" style="display:none">How does one go about writing a review of one’s favourite book of all? One runs the risk of making one of two errors: one might either ignore flaws, and praise too much, to the point of gushing; or, one might try and be too critical in an attempt to be even-handed, and thus sell the work short.<br /><br />Mikhail Sholokhov’s Quiet Flows the Don (that’s the complete and unabridged English edition of Tikhii Don, “The Quiet Don” in the original Russian) is not an easy book to read. It’s not something for those who have limited attention spans and/or no knowledge of history. And, though it’s a novel of war, it’s emphatically not for those who are looking for action. <br /><br />The Russian Civil War was one of the most defining events of history. This is not the place for discussing its effects, but two things should be remembered: first, if it had not happened, the twentieth century would have been almost in every way completely different; and, secondly, the victory of the Bolsheviks in the war was far from assured. As the war progressed, the Red Army had not only to fight against the Whites, but against anarchists, assorted bandit formations, and rebellious Cossacks, who switched sides several times as the tide of events washed over them.<br /><br />The book is the story of one such Cossack, and of the women whom he loves, and the struggles he has to go through.<br /><br />Grigorii Melekhov is a young Cossack in the Don valley just prior to the start of the First World War. He loves Aksinia, the wife of another Cossack, and this results in so much fighting and turmoil that his father forcibly marries him off to another woman, Natalya, who is as sweet and gentle as Aksinia is fiery and determined. Finally, Aksinia and Grigorii run off together, and find employment in the mansion of a wealthy landowner, where they might find a modicum of happiness.<br /><br />But the war comes, the First World War, and the Tsar orders the Cossacks to the front. Grigorii is sent to the Austro-Hungarian front, and for the first time kills men in combat. Meanwhile, as the war drags on, the Tsarist autocracy begins to collapse under the weight of its own corruption, and the masses begin to rise up in protest.<br /><br />The Don Cossacks were not directly involved in the overthrow of the monarchy, and the news only filtered through to them later, causing anxiety more than anything. After all, though they had been routinely used as cannon fodder by the Tsars, they were historically and almost organically part of the Tsarist apparatus, and could not imagine life under any other system. However, they weren’t exactly given a choice in the matter, and soon enough the tides of the Civil War washed over them.<br /><br />Quite apart from not being a book for the attention-span-challenged, this book is not for the squeamish; the Russian Civil War was one of the most brutal in history, with both sides (or, to be exact, all sides) routinely massacring prisoners and carrying out what today would be considered war crimes. In this war, there were two kinds of combatant; one who was ideologically driven, and fought on one or the other side, through victory or defeat; and, on the other, the kind of soldier who fought not for ideology but in defence of his own ethnic group and its perceived interests. Grigorii Melekhov was emphatically of the latter, and switched back and forth from the Red to the White sides as the fortunes of war dictated.<br /><br />Meanwhile, the two women in his life, Natalya and Aksinia, live through their own private torment – Natalya, scarred in a suicide attempt, her attempt to make a life again with Grigorii sabotaged by the war; and Aksinia, torn between her husband, whom she does not love and who does not love her, and Grigorii, whose child she has borne and who she knows is torn between her and Natalya. It’s a love story as well as a war story, and in these circumstances the outcome can’t be anything but tragic.<br /><br />The writing, even in translation, is superb, the minor characters brilliantly etched. It’s not a book which one can read and say to oneself, “Now so-and-so is quite superfluous; one could just delete him and nothing would be lost”. Even the insignificant valet of the landowner has his own place in the narrative, serving to fill out the canvas. And there’s the odd moment where one sees the hope of the future, like the German whom Grigorii takes prisoner early in the novel and then lets go, who shakes his hand and tells him: “In the coming class wars we’ll be on the same side of the barricades, won’t we, comrade?”<br /><br />One of the most important things about the novel is its sympathetic treatment of the Whites; though written in Stalin’s USSR by a very much establishment author, it’s as far from a Communist polemic as it’s possible to get. That it won the Stalin Prize in 1941 is another proof of something I’ve said before and will say again: the alleged persecution of dissent by Stalin is at least partly exaggerated, not least by his successors like Khrushchev for their own political purposes.<br /><br />What are the flaws of the book? Apart from the fact that one needs at least some background knowledge of the First World War and the Russian Civil War, including an idea of Russian civil society of the time, at two thick volumes it’s certainly not something to be gone through in one session. Spanning almost a decade, the ebb and flow of characters can sometimes be confusing, especially if one is a newcomer to Russian literature and can’t remember Russian names. <br /><br />Also, the only conclusion one can draw from this book is, when brothers fight, nobody wins. Even the victor has lost whatever it was that was worth fighting for.<br /><br />Completely and absolutely recommended, for those who care.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5721314962282062980" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2989530519862791707">In Russian, the most beautifully written and original prose I've ever read, by a mile. The language is simply stunning and makes even inanimate objects appear as alive as most human characters in average novels. It depicts the life of ordinary but fierce and colorfu</span>
  <span id="freeText2989530519862791707" style="display:none">In Russian, the most beautifully written and original prose I've ever read, by a mile. The language is simply stunning and makes even inanimate objects appear as alive as most human characters in average novels. It would be a monumental task to translate it into other languages, I suspect..<br /><br />Aside from the language, it's an epic read about a very interesting people in a crucial time in Russian history -- Cossacks during the Russian Revolution. It depicts the life of ordinary but fierce and colorful characters trying to make it through a merciless historical tsunami. Passion, need for freedom, all-consuming civil war, people of great earthly spirit who are willing to endure suffering but not submission.<br /><br /></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_32270934" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/32270934" />
    <a title="Bettie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1108123-bettie"><img alt="Bettie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602504374p2/1108123.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/32270934?book_show_action=true">Sep 07, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bettie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bettie" href="/user/show/1108123-bettie">Bettie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1108123-bettie?shelf=paper-read">paper-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1108123-bettie?shelf=published-1929">published-1929</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1108123-bettie?shelf=nobel-laureate">nobel-laureate</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1108123-bettie?shelf=teh-brillianz">teh-brillianz</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer3815503090306270888">This is a really strange book - it is in four parts. The first two are brilliant hardyesque descriptions of peasant life in peace and war. Part three sees the novel's structure collapse as the Russian Revolution takes hold. Sholokov was clearly obliged to incorporate much Marxist theory and the tone becomes quite abstract. The final part sees a return to the countryside, and an inconclusive finish. <br/><br/>The only consistent progression in this novel is the role and treatment of women. In the opening c</span>
  <span id="freeText3815503090306270888" style="display:none">This is a really strange book - it is in four parts. The first two are brilliant hardyesque descriptions of peasant life in peace and war. Part three sees the novel's structure collapse as the Russian Revolution takes hold. Sholokov was clearly obliged to incorporate much Marxist theory and the tone becomes quite abstract. The final part sees a return to the countryside, and an inconclusive finish. <br /><br />The only consistent progression in this novel is the role and treatment of women. In the opening chapters women are beaten and even lynched for sorcery. By the end they are taking their equal part in the revolution as soldiers standing alongside the men.</span>

<div id="review_1424724830" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1424724830" />
    <a title="Ksenia Bazan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/42182526-ksenia-bazan"><img alt="Ksenia Bazan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1559499998p2/42182526.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1424724830?book_show_action=true">Oct 25, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ksenia Bazan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ksenia Bazan" href="/user/show/42182526-ksenia-bazan">Ksenia Bazan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42182526-ksenia-bazan?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42182526-ksenia-bazan?shelf=russian-literature">russian-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1424724830" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18272639405620732010">Very late at night I reached the end of this unique epic book. Russian literature treasure awarded by the Nobel prize totally deserves to be called one of the best novels of the XXth century.<br/>It starts with the First book describing a peaceful living of several Cossack families and Sholokhov makes two people front and center of the story: Grigori and Aksinia. All I can say is that I'm in love with them both. Two amazing realistic characters, flawed, passionate, logical, sometimes illogical. They </span>
  <span id="freeText18272639405620732010" style="display:none">Very late at night I reached the end of this unique epic book. Russian literature treasure awarded by the Nobel prize totally deserves to be called one of the best novels of the XXth century.<br />It starts with the First book describing a peaceful living of several Cossack families and Sholokhov makes two people front and center of the story: Grigori and Aksinia. All I can say is that I'm in love with them both. Two amazing realistic characters, flawed, passionate, logical, sometimes illogical. They actually do the things real people could do in their places. The first of the quartet of books is all about life, introducing normal life as it was before the War got it affected. By far it is my most beloved book of four of them. <br />The second book takes such a step away from the first one that you are really asking the question, wait is it the continuation? Yes, it is. The main theme of this part is World War I and the rising Soviet power. Sholokhov shows us Grigori in the middle of events. He is showing how the revolution happened in minds of tired soldiers dying in the field. Here I had a tiny problem but after reading the whole book I find the author made it right that he shares only some scenes with Aksinia in center.<br />The third book is about the Civil War. The character development of Grigori is overwhelming. We grow wiser with him, he is mature, we can definitely see that he was a boy in the beginning but now he is such a manly man. <br />The fourth book is about fighting for your own life. Before the protagonist was fighing for both sides, hesitating, coming from side to side. But now he wants just to be alive, to live with the loved ones, to come away from all those world problems.<br /><br />I recommend this book for those history fans, who want to look beyond, for those who are looking for a good love story and those who want to know Russian culture from the inside.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18272639405620732010" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review1424724830">

<span id="freeTextContainer1374036031036265749">I found a translation by Robert Daglish which was revised and edited by Brian Murphy. This edition was published by Carroll & Graf in 1996. The ISBN is: 0786703601. J. M. Dent published it in the UK.<br/>I've been wanting to read this since high school, when my father would occasionally refer to a great book he'd read years before. At that time, the English translation of the title was AND QUIET FLOWS THE DON. He said it was about a Russian soldier driven to the point of exhaustion. He added that he </span>
  <span id="freeText1374036031036265749" style="display:none">I found a translation by Robert Daglish which was revised and edited by Brian Murphy. This edition was published by Carroll &amp; Graf in 1996. The ISBN is: 0786703601. J. M. Dent published it in the UK.<br />I've been wanting to read this since high school, when my father would occasionally refer to a great book he'd read years before. At that time, the English translation of the title was AND QUIET FLOWS THE DON. He said it was about a Russian soldier driven to the point of exhaustion. He added that he thought the Soviet authorities had slipped in propaganda, because, after sections of tremendous beauty, absurd breastbeating would appear.<br />Sometime in the eighties I read an article by Alexander Solzhenitsyn, asserting that this novel was indeed tampered with by the Soviet government.<br />I sense that the '96 translation and revision has restored some of the author's original work. I have not yet read the introduction, which will lay out the editor's method, but I'm looking forward to it.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1936166914" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1936166914" />
    <a title="Czarny Pies" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29160274-czarny-pies"><img alt="Czarny Pies" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1400687408p2/29160274.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1936166914?book_show_action=true">Mar 13, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Czarny Pies" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Czarny Pies" href="/user/show/29160274-czarny-pies">Czarny Pies</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">No one.  If you are curious about the King of Social Realism read the Virgin Soil Upturned instead.</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29160274-czarny-pies?shelf=russian-lit">russian-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29160274-czarny-pies?shelf=nobel-prize-winners">nobel-prize-winners</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1936166914" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10280267891801080841">Mo Yan the 2012 Nobel Laureate and prime apologist for the Chinese Communist regime considers Mikhail Sholokhov to have been one the great writers of the 20th Century. In Yan's view Sholokhov is the great master at showing the enormous difficulty in implanting a truly working class world view in a country in the wake of a communist revolution. One should then acquaint oneself with Sholokhov not for the literary quality of his work but because he is representative of a major historical phenomenon</span>
  <span id="freeText10280267891801080841" style="display:none">Mo Yan the 2012 Nobel Laureate and prime apologist for the Chinese Communist regime considers Mikhail Sholokhov to have been one the great writers of the 20th Century. In Yan's view Sholokhov is the great master at showing the enormous difficulty in implanting a truly working class world view in a country in the wake of a communist revolution. One should then acquaint oneself with Sholokhov not for the literary quality of his work but because he is representative of a major historical phenomenon which is still ongoing.<br /><br />However, I am by no means convinced that "And Quiet Flows the Don" which is generally considered to be his masterpiece is the best work to read in order to get acquainted with this giant of the social realism genre. <br /><br />In Volume One, Sholokhov takes 650 pages to tell a story that could just as well have been told in 150 pages. The characters are all quite repulsive. The men are brawlers and rapists. The women are promiscuous routinely tacking lovers whenever their husbands depart to do their military service or conduct other business away from home. Worse members of both sexes are highly committed to the status quo. They are neither able nor willing to analyze their societyin marxist terms. A visiting communist proselytizer has virtually no success in raising class consciousness. Volume One reminds one very much of Emile Zola's "La Terre" which also deals with the theme of the primitiveness, brutality and all pervasive stupidity of peasant life in Europe in the years prior to WWI. However next to Zola, Sholokhov is altogether too earnest and pedantic.<br /><br />"And Quiet Flows the Don" improves after this rather dismal Volume One. Nonetheless<br />I would recommend to anyone curious about Sholokhov to read instead "Virgin Soil Upturned" which is a fascinating portrait of the collectivization of Ukrainian agriculture prior to WWII. The characters are more engaging and the plot is dramatically superior.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10280267891801080841" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joel Palma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joel Palma" href="/user/show/35104293-joel-palma">Joel Palma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35104293-joel-palma?shelf=nobel-prize-in-literature">nobel-prize-in-literature</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1614789824" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12309643439229022148">Impressive!!! <br/><br/>I've been reading a lot of reviews that this book by Sholokhov- the second Russian Nobel Prize in Literature laureate, can ONLY be compared to Tolstoy's "War and Peace"... I braced myself to this premise, not because it's daunting but because (after reading War and Peace, one of my four favorite novels of all time- to date) I'm in for an unforgettable literary roller coaster ride- and Sholokhov did not disappoint!!! Like "War and Peace" this book is massive, ambitious and demanding</span>
  <span id="freeText12309643439229022148" style="display:none">Impressive!!! <br /><br />I've been reading a lot of reviews that this book by Sholokhov- the second Russian Nobel Prize in Literature laureate, can ONLY be compared to Tolstoy's "War and Peace"... I braced myself to this premise, not because it's daunting but because (after reading War and Peace, one of my four favorite novels of all time- to date) I'm in for an unforgettable literary roller coaster ride- and Sholokhov did not disappoint!!! Like "War and Peace" this book is massive, ambitious and demanding! I even watched two documentaries about the Russian Revolution and looked up real person characters included in this book like Kaledin and Kerensky. Since this book is a "big deal" in literary world, I made it a point to really give time to fully digest each and every page. Tip: read it slowly but continuously (daily/regularly), the numbers of characters (not to mention the intimidating Russian names) can be confusing. The revolution and civil war chapters are complex and oftentimes (I feel) fast paced and shifty but nonetheless an amazing read! <br /><br />5 stars!</span>
  <a data-text-id="12309643439229022148" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1539265368" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8855080383959414084">One of the best books I have ever read. I have always loved Russian authors, and Sholokhov is definitely up there with Tolstoy, and Dostoevsky.<br/>I can understand this being likened to War and Peace. Very similar, just set in a different era. It's a beautifully written book, but heartbreaking as well. <br/>I knew nothing about Cossacks before I read this, and now I definitely want to know more</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2924769741" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2924769741" />
    <a title="Tarek Amer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24867387-tarek-amer"><img alt="Tarek Amer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1575440328p2/24867387.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2924769741?book_show_action=true">Aug 15, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tarek Amer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tarek Amer" href="/user/show/24867387-tarek-amer">Tarek Amer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 9782843061929" href="/book/show/25317323">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2924769741" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11683618177686693325">The plot begins with the World War in a small village by the river Don. Grigori Melekhov lives there with his family that consists of his parents, sister and a married brother.<br/><br/>Grigori is in a love affair with a married woman called Aksinia. Her husband Stephan is in the army. The traditional people didn’t approve of their relationship and because of that Grigori’s father, Panteley, arranges his son to be married to Natalya. She came from a rich family.<br/><br/>Grigori married her but he wasn’t happy. She</span>
  <span id="freeText11683618177686693325" style="display:none">The plot begins with the World War in a small village by the river Don. Grigori Melekhov lives there with his family that consists of his parents, sister and a married brother.<br /><br />Grigori is in a love affair with a married woman called Aksinia. Her husband Stephan is in the army. The traditional people didn’t approve of their relationship and because of that Grigori’s father, Panteley, arranges his son to be married to Natalya. She came from a rich family.<br /><br />Grigori married her but he wasn’t happy. She was a good and loyal wife but a bit cold. After a few years, Grigori went back to Aksinia.<br /><br />The first communist supporters started to appear in the village and they were fighting for equality.<br /><br />Grigori ran away with Aksinia and they worked on a field of a retired military officer. In the meantime, Natayla gave birth to Grigori’s child and demanded him to leave Aksinia and take care of them. Grigori refused to do it so Natalya tried to kill herself.<br /><br />The war started and Grigori was mobilized into the army. In the beginning, he felt ill from the killing and the blood but soon he got though. He was even wounded several times and because of his great courage, he was honored.<br /><br />Soon after his daughter Tanya died from diphtheria. Aksinia was alone and desperate and in that condition the retired officer’s son seduced her. When Grigori found out about it he ended his relationship with Aksinia. After that Grigori confronted the officer’s son and decided to go back to the village. He also came back to Natalya.<br /><br />The war was raging on and the military was dissatisfied. All of the mentioned became a good territory for the revolutionary propaganda to be spread across Russia. One Bolshevik told that the war against the Germans will turn into a civil war. In the meantime, Natalya gave birth to a boy and a girl.<br /><br />The army got more and more tired and when everyone believed that the war is over a civil war started. The October revolution began and Grigori joined the Bolsheviks. His honesty had a lot to do with it because he wanted to fight off the inequality and injustice. Even though he believes in his ideals they fell apart soon.<br /><br />Grigori was struck by the murder of two officers from the opposite side. They were killed by the leader of the revolutionary movement.<br /><br />When he came back home he decided to fight against the Bolsheviks. But he wasn’t happy either because murders were still going on in each side of the civil war.<br /><br />In the meantime, the officer’s son got married and abandoned Aksinia. She came back to her husband Stephan who was liberated from captivity. Natalya was pregnant again and the child was Grigori’s but she didn’t want to be pregnant. She tried to violently end the pregnancy and she ended up dead.<br /><br />Grigori was demobilized and came back home. There he met his son-in-law who was leading the Bolshevik revolution in his village.<br /><br />Grigori joined a gang but soon he abandoned it and ran away with Aksinia. They were ambushed and Aksinia was killed. Grigori was broken down and while he was burying her body he realized how sad and unfair life is.<br /><br />The story brings a historic event, a revolution and a passionate love affair between Grigori and Aksinia. The author tried to show us a sad faith in times of war. This novel tells us about history but at the same time it takes on a journey through a life of an ordinary man.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11683618177686693325" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2612010436" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2612010436" />
    <a title="Richard Newton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19540749-richard-newton"><img alt="Richard Newton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1562420591p2/19540749.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2612010436?book_show_action=true">Dec 23, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard Newton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard Newton" href="/user/show/19540749-richard-newton">Richard Newton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19540749-richard-newton?shelf=fiction-and-literature">fiction-and-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19540749-richard-newton?shelf=central-asia-to-eastern-europe">central-asia-to-eastern-europe</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2612010436" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8053371121603904805">Disappointing, and I may have been generous with my 3 stars. (For clarity I read the Penguin Modern Classics edition - ISBN 9780241284407 translated by Stephen Garry. I make the edition clear as I suspect there may be better ones out there, but I do not know this for certain).<br/><br/>This book is occasionally excellent and it certainly captures the randomness of a revolution and civil war where people choose sides almost by chance - and suffer accordingly. The common man never really had a view of which</span>
  <span id="freeText8053371121603904805" style="display:none">Disappointing, and I may have been generous with my 3 stars. (For clarity I read the Penguin Modern Classics edition - ISBN 9780241284407 translated by Stephen Garry. I make the edition clear as I suspect there may be better ones out there, but I do not know this for certain).<br /><br />This book is occasionally excellent and it certainly captures the randomness of a revolution and civil war where people choose sides almost by chance - and suffer accordingly. The common man never really had a view of which side would do what so any choice was flawed. It tells the story of the Don Cossacks - and generally it is a rather unpleasant story of unpleasant people doing occasionally heroic things. Rape and violence are common place. Bigoted views abound. I did not like any of the characters. But this is probably a realistic portrayal of rustic life in much of the world in that era. <br /><br />Nevertheless I think there is an occasionally great book struggling to show itself here. <br /><br />The problem is that firstly the translation is poor. For instance, the translator talks of horses and food "smoking", when I am told by my wife, a fluent Russian speaker, that the same verb could have been translated at "steaming". Calling pasta "vermicelli" is ridiculous and completely out of cultural context. A custom at a wedding of saying "bitter, bitter", which I happen to know is said to the marriage couple to get them to kiss, is not explained. Literally translating it as "bitter" is correct, but unhelpful to a native English speaker. Perhaps something like "sweeten it with a kiss" or similar. There are lots of similar small examples which irritate rather than damage the story. But I prefer not to be irritated as I read!<br /><br />The second problem is that the story does not flow. At times it seems to make huge random jumps. Given this book is 566 pages, albeit of rather small font, and other versions are over 1000 I think this might be abridged. However, that is not stated anywhere. If it is - the translator has abridged it very badly. If not, its a poorly structured book. This really is the main problem. <br /><br />So if you can bring yourself to plough your way through several hundred pages of misguidedly translated prose, for some occasionally excellent parts and a good picture of the revolution in an otherwise slightly random story - go ahead. But then, perhaps you might be better off looking for a different version/translation if there is one out there. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8053371121603904805" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2651609676" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2651609676" />
    <a title="Ila" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56761305-ila"><img alt="Ila" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546267699p2/56761305.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2651609676" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17685255193645262186">3 stars. I only read volume 1 as it was what I stipulated in my book challenge. <br/><br/>Sholokhov describes the world his characters inhabit in a manner reminiscent of Tolstoy, although there is no glamour and enchantment a la Russian aristocracy. I barely knew anything about the Cossacks and the civil war; I learnt a lot in the process. The language is engaging and unadorned with the occasional stunning sentence. I had expected a fair deal of violence but the graphic depictions of rape demands a strong</span>
  <span id="freeText17685255193645262186" style="display:none">3 stars. I only read volume 1 as it was what I stipulated in my book challenge. <br /><br />Sholokhov describes the world his characters inhabit in a manner reminiscent of Tolstoy, although there is no glamour and enchantment a la Russian aristocracy. I barely knew anything about the Cossacks and the civil war; I learnt a lot in the process. The language is engaging and unadorned with the occasional stunning sentence. I had expected a fair deal of violence but the graphic depictions of rape demands a strong stomach from the reader. There are some lovely songs but the novel suffers mightily in character development. Stepan and Aksinia are some of the most insipid characters I have read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17685255193645262186" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Wouter Janssen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Wouter Janssen" href="/user/show/89248206-wouter-janssen">Wouter Janssen</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2914430089" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14927538507434519401">A thrilling account of the rural Don cossacks during the First World War, the Bolshevik Revolution and the overthrowing of the Tsar and the subsequent civil war between the Red Guards and the counter-revolutionaries, all the while the gentle Don quietly flows through the steppe. What an epic and great and stunning and legendary book! </span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('93W/L8jtVaSVvomg0RTtnamo7OEH65aJpdwM8sV5xiOISyr1sfb+13cOBOL/hzTVW9czyyW/sEC1YNBPPbkVrw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('EZLO315AfrGj0Gl2IcC74b/nRb9XT0A3Ec65GQGKx5NurFsFJ1vVwkFg5DQPU2KpTZialXUbZv4BcmWk+UoUHw==')})
  }

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_178196'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/178196.What_Is_to_Be_Done_?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">What Is to Be Done?<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/3424683.Nikolai_Chernyshevsky?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Nikolai Chernyshevsky<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.44 avg rating &mdash; 2,047 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1863\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer11054691532546820437\">\"No work in modern literature, with the possible exception of Uncle Tom\'s Cabin, can compete with What Is to Be Done? in its effect on human lives and its power to make history. For Chernyshevsky\'s novel, far more than Marx\'s Capital, supplied the em<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText11054691532546820437\" style=\"display:none\">&quot;No work in modern literature, with the possible exception of Uncle Tom\'s Cabin, can compete with What Is to Be Done? in its effect on human lives and its power to make history. For Chernyshevsky\'s novel, far more than Marx\'s Capital, supplied the emotional dynamic that eventually went to make the Russian Revolution.&quot;--Joseph Frank, The Southern Review\n\nAlmost from the moment of its publication in 1863, Nikolai Chernyshevsky\'s novel, What Is to Be Done?, had a profound impact on the course of Russian literature and politics. The idealized image it offered of dedicated and self-sacrificing intellectuals transforming society by means of scientific knowledge served as a model of inspiration for Russia\'s revolutionary intelligentsia. On the one hand, the novel\'s condemnation of moderate reform helped to bring about the irrevocable break between radical intellectuals and liberal reformers; on the other, Chernyshevsky\'s socialist vision polarized conservatives\' opposition to institutional reform. Lenin himself called Chernyshevsky &quot;the greatest and most talented representative of socialism before Marx&quot;; and the controversy surrounding What Is to Be Done? exacerbated the conflicts that eventually led to the Russian Revolution.\n\nMichael R. Katz\'s readable and compelling translation is now the definitive unabridged English-language version, brilliantly capturing the extraordinary qualities of the original. William G. Wagner has provided full annotations to Chernyshevsky\'s allusions and references and to the, sources of his ideas, and has appended a critical bibliography. المحار، وحافة رمادية اللون منكسرة قوامها الحصباء تلثمها الأمواج، ومن ثم وجه الدون الرقراق الفولاذي اللون، تلاعبه الريح، وإلى الشرق، وراء أسيجة من أغصان الصفصاف تحيط بساحات درس الحبوب، يمتد طريق هتمان، حيث يوجد نبات الشيح الرمادي، ونبات لسان الحمل المتصلب ذو اللون البني والذي هرسته حوافز الخيل، وثمة مصلى عند مفترق الطريق، ثم يأتي السهب مغلفاً بضباب متنقل، وإلى الجنوب، تمتد سلسلة من التلال الطباشرية، وإلى الغرب، هناك الشارع يقطع الساحة ميمماً صوب المروج الساحلية.\n\nعاد القوفازي بروكوفي ميليخوف إلى القرية أثناء الحرب ما قبل الأخيرة مع تركيا، وقد جلب معه زوجة: إمرأة صغيرة متلفعة بشال من رأسها إلى قدمها، تعطي وجهها على الدوام، ونادراً ما تكشف عن عينيها الوحشيتين الحزينتين، وكان الشال الحريري يحمل عطوراً غريبة فواحة، وطرائزه الملونة بألوان قوس تثير حسد القوقازيات.\n\nوظلت هذه الأسيرة التركية بعيدة عن اقارب بروكوفي، ولم يمض وقت طويل حتى أعطى ميليخوف العجوز لولده حصته من الإرث، وظل طيلة حياته يرفض أن يضع قدمه داخل بيت ابنه، ذلك لأنه لم يستطع أن ينسى ذاك العار، وسرعان ما تدبر بروكوفي أمره، فأقام له البخارون داراً، وصنع هو بنفسه سياجاً لساحة الماشية، وفي مطلع الخريف أخذ زوجته الأجنبية محنية الرأس إلى دارها الجديدة.\n\nوقد سار معها خلال القرية وراء العربة المحملة بما لديها من متاع الدنيا، فاندفع الجميع، شيباً وشباناً، إلى الشارع، وكتم الرجال ضحكاتهم وراء لحاهم، وتناقلت النسوة تعليقات مسموعة، بينما اندفع سرب من أطفال القوقاز الوسخين يزعقون خلف بروكوفي، ولكنه واصل سيره بطيئاً، بمعطفه مفتوح الأزرار، وكأنه يترسم أرضاً حدثت توّاً، وهو يعصر معصم زوجته الرقيق بكفه السوداء الضخمة، رافعاً رأسه ذا الناصية الشقراء، في تحدٍّ وإستخفاف، سوى أن أكياسه الدهنية تحت عظام خديه انتفخت وارتعشت، وتفصد العرق بين حاجبيه المقطبين دائماً.\n\nمنذ ذلك الحين لم يقع عليه نظر إنسان إلا نادراً، ولم يحضر هو لقاءات القوقاز بناتاً، وعاش حياة عزلة في داره المنفردة على جانب الدون، ورويت عنه حكايات غريبة في القرية... مثل ظهور المجلد الأول من &quot;الدون الهادئ&quot; حدثاً كبيراً للأدب السوفييتي الفتي بأسره فقد كان ميخائيل شولوخوف عند صدوره في الثالثة والعشرين من عمره، وقال عنه وبعد قراءته لهذا المجلد للدون الهادئ &quot;أنه يكتب كقوزاقي عاشق للدون، ولحياة القوزاق - وللطبيعة&quot;.\n\nفي رواية شولوخوف هذه بالذات، انفتح إقليم الدون على الأدب العالمي بشكل حقيقي، وأصبحت منطقة الدون جزءاً لا يمكن فصله عن اسم شولوخوف، مثلما لا يمكن فصل الدنيبو عن غوغول، منطقة ريازين عن يسينين، والغولغا عن غوركي.\n\nوإلى هذا فإن &quot;الدون الهادئ&quot; هو ذات قيمة هائلة للمؤرخ والمختص بالإقتصاد السياسي، والفيسلوف، ولعالم الإجتماع، ومع ذلك فإن كشف الفنان الكبير للنفس الإنسانية هو أعلى من كل الحقائق الأخرى، إن المعرفة بالخلق القوزاقي، والعادات، والقوة المالية، والمطامح الرفيعة لهؤلاء، ليس بمجموعهم فقط، بل ولكل شخص على إنفراد، هي وحدها تتيح للقارئ الفهم فهماً كاملاً لماذا كان من الممكن أن ينشأ، وتحت سقف واحد، أشخاص متباينون، مثل بيتر ميليخوف، وغروغوري وميليخوف، ومثل ميتكا الجزار وناتاليا الطاهرة النفس من آل كورشو نوف.\n\nومن ناحية الشكل الفني، غالباً ما يصنف &quot;دون الهادئ&quot; كملحمة شعبية، وعلى هذا المستوى يجب أن يفهم كل شيء في هذا العمل، بمجلداته الأربعة، تضم الرواية أكثر من 600 شخصية وجميعهم على مستوى من الأهمية، فمن خلالهم بإمكان القارئ فهم وبعمق وبشكل صادق تلك الشخصية المعقدة من الشعب، الباحثة بعذاب عن موقعها في الثورة، فغريغوري ميليخوف، الشخصية المحورية، مرتبط، بهذا الشكل أو بالآخر، أو بتلك الشخصية، أو هذه الأخرى، وحتى بأولئك الذين لم يلتق بهم بشكل مباشر أبداً، في تعقيدات الأحداث العاصفة.\n\nوعلى خلفية مصائر كل هذه الشخصيات الستمائة في &quot;الدون الهادئ&quot; (وشخصيات مثل بونتشوك أو ميشكا كوشيفوي قبل كل شيء) يمكن فهم تاريخ حياة غريفوري نفسه، تلك الفكرة العميقة التي وضعها الروائي في هذه الشخصية الرائعة حقاً.\n\nومع مرور السنين، تكشف رواية شولوخوف أمام القارئ المزيد والمزيد من أبعادها الجديدة ذات الدلالة، كما المزيد من المواضيع والقضايا والصراعات والمشاكل الإجتماعية والمعاشية المحضة التي لم يلحظها النقاد من قبل، ولا بد من القول بأن ما زال هناك وإلى الآن جدل إبداعي يدور حول &quot;الدون الهادئ&quot; منذ العام 1928 لا ينقطع، فالرواية هي نفسها، إلا أن الأسئلة التي تبرز في مجرى النقاش، في العقود المتعاقبة تبدو مختلفة.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"1976820053896032961\" الرقراق الفولاذي اللون، تلاعبه الريح، وإلى الشرق، وراء أسيجة من أغصان الصفصاف تحيط بساحات درس الحبوب، يمتد طريق هتمان، حيث يوجد نبات الشيح الرمادي، ونبات لسان الحمل المتصلب ذو اللون البني والذي هرسته حوافز الخيل، وثمة مصلى عند مفترق الطريق، ثم يأتي السهب مغلفاً بضباب متنقل، وإلى الجنوب، تمتد سلسلة من التلال الطباشرية، وإلى الغرب، هناك الشارع يقطع الساحة ميمماً صوب المروج الساحلية.\n\nعاد القوفازي بروكوفي ميليخوف إلى القرية أثناء الحرب ما قبل الأخيرة مع تركيا، وقد جلب معه زوجة: إمرأة صغيرة متلفعة بشال من رأسها إلى قدمها، تعطي وجهها على الدوام، ونادراً ما تكشف عن عينيها الوحشيتين الحزينتين، وكان الشال الحريري يحمل عطوراً غريبة فواحة، وطرائزه الملونة بألوان قوس تثير حسد القوقازيات.\n\nوظلت هذه الأسيرة التركية بعيدة عن اقارب بروكوفي، ولم يمض وقت طويل حتى أعطى ميليخوف العجوز لولده حصته من الإرث، وظل طيلة حياته يرفض أن يضع قدمه داخل بيت ابنه، ذلك لأنه لم يستطع أن ينسى ذاك العار، وسرعان ما تدبر بروكوفي أمره، فأقام له البخارون داراً، وصنع هو بنفسه سياجاً لساحة الماشية، وفي مطلع الخريف أخذ زوجته الأجنبية محنية الرأس إلى دارها الجديدة.\n\nوقد سار معها خلال القرية وراء العربة المحملة بما لديها من متاع الدنيا، فاندفع الجميع، شيباً وشباناً، إلى الشارع، وكتم الرجال ضحكاتهم وراء لحاهم، وتناقلت النسوة تعليقات مسموعة، بينما اندفع سرب من أطفال القوقاز الوسخين يزعقون خلف بروكوفي، ولكنه واصل سيره بطيئاً، بمعطفه مفتوح الأزرار، وكأنه يترسم أرضاً حدثت توّاً، وهو يعصر معصم زوجته الرقيق بكفه السوداء الضخمة، رافعاً رأسه ذا الناصية الشقراء، في تحدٍّ وإستخفاف، سوى أن أكياسه الدهنية تحت عظام خديه انتفخت وارتعشت، وتفصد العرق بين حاجبيه المقطبين دائماً.\n\nمنذ ذلك الحين لم يقع عليه نظر إنسان إلا The first three volumes were written between 1925-32 and published in the Soviet magazine <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText7775901598610107127\" style=\"display:none\">And Quiet Flows the Don or Quietly Flows the Don (Тихий Дон, lit. &quot;The Quiet Don&quot;) is a 4-volume epic novel by Russian writer Mikhail Aleksandrovich Sholokhov. The first three volumes were written between 1925-32 and published in the Soviet magazine \'October\'. The 4th volume was finished in 1940. The English translation of the first three volumes appeared under this title in 1934. The novel is considered one of the most significant works of Russian literature in the 20th century. It depicts the lives and struggles of the Don Cossacks during WWI, the Russian Revolution &amp; Russian Civil War. In 1965, Sholokhov was awarded the Nobel Prize for Literature for this novel.
